  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c188e684-c1c0-412d-829f-54a739095154'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

              <span id="reviewTextContainer384380555" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11136540662512636074">155.	Jazz, Toni Morrison<br/>Jazz is a 1992 historical novel by Pulitzer and Nobel Prize-winning American author Toni Morrison. The majority of the narrative takes place in Harlem during the 1920s; however, as the pasts of the various characters are explored, the narrative extends back to the mid-19th-century American South. The novel forms the second part of Morrison's Dantesque trilogy on African-American history, beginning with Beloved (1987) and ending with Paradise (1997). <br/><br/>عنوانها: جاز ؛ نویسنده</span>
  <span id="freeText11136540662512636074" style="display:none">155.	Jazz, Toni Morrison<br />Jazz is a 1992 historical novel by Pulitzer and Nobel Prize-winning American author Toni Morrison. The majority of the narrative takes place in Harlem during the 1920s; however, as the pasts of the various characters are explored, the narrative extends back to the mid-19th-century American South. The novel forms the second part of Morrison's Dantesque trilogy on African-American history, beginning with Beloved (1987) and ending with Paradise (1997). <br /><br />عنوانها: جاز ؛ نویسنده: تونی موریسون؛ نشر (آفرینه) ادبیات آمریکا؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز پنجم ماه آگوست سال 2002 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: جاز؛ نویسنده: تونی موریسون؛ مترجم: سهیل سمی؛ تهران، آفرینه، 1379؛ در 240 ص؛ شابک: 9646191444؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان امریکایی - سده 20 م<br /><br />سبک پسامدرن، با زبانی استعاری، توصیفی از جامعه رنگین پوستان امریكایی، در دهه های بیست و سی، از سده ی نوزدهم میلادی، روایتی از نسلی که با پشت سر نهادن جنگ، و درگیرشدن با پیامدهاییش، در جستجوی فضایی آرام برای زیستن هستند. ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_1483210613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1483210613" />
    <a title="Candi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3639005-candi"><img alt="Candi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585187464p2/3639005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1483210613?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Candi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Candi" href="/user/show/3639005-candi">Candi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=african-american">african-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=book-i-own">book-i-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1483210613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13529896599924877741">2 stars<br/><br/><i>"I'm crazy about this City. Daylight slants like a razor cutting the buildings in half. In the top half I see looking faces and it's not easy to tell which are people, which the work of stonemasons. Below is a shadow where any blasé thing takes place: clarinets and lovemaking, fists and the voices of sorrowful women. A city like this one makes me dream tall and feel in on things."</i><br/><br/>Oh, how I adore the lyricism of Toni Morrison. I have had <b>Jazz</b> on my shelf for quite some time now, and follow</span>
  <span id="freeText13529896599924877741" style="display:none">2 stars<br /><br /><i>"I'm crazy about this City. Daylight slants like a razor cutting the buildings in half. In the top half I see looking faces and it's not easy to tell which are people, which the work of stonemasons. Below is a shadow where any blasé thing takes place: clarinets and lovemaking, fists and the voices of sorrowful women. A city like this one makes me dream tall and feel in on things."</i><br /><br />Oh, how I adore the lyricism of Toni Morrison. I have had <b>Jazz</b> on my shelf for quite some time now, and following my admiration of <b>The Bluest Eye</b>, <b>Sula</b>, and <b>Beloved</b>, I couldn't wait to tear into this one. The title alone captured my imagination. Unfortunately, I was left feeling a bit confused and underwhelmed. <br /><br />Set during the Jazz Age in New York City, this book had great potential. An intriguing plot description – man has an affair with a younger woman, man shoots and kills his lover (not a spoiler), man's wife attacks corpse of this young woman at her funeral! This is where the story begins and I was immediately hooked. However, just as I latched onto the narrative, it would shift. The voices changed frequently; time and setting changed often. It now became apparent that the title of the story had less to do with any musical plot than it did with the perhaps experimental style of writing. I am not an expert in jazz music, but I am definitely a fan. From Wikipedia, jazz is defined as "a spontaneity and vitality of musical production in which improvisation plays a role and contains a sonority and manner of phrasing which mirror the individuality of the performing jazz musician". I can imagine each voice in this story as a different instrument in a jazz ensemble. However, the shift from one voice or instrument to the next was too jolting, too disjointed. While this may work for me in music, I suppose I prefer a writing that flows more harmoniously and smoothly. <br /><br />There were moments of brilliance shining through, as I have come to expect from this extremely gifted author. When characters were engaged in dialogue, I was immersed in the story. But the stream of consciousness feel to this book did not appeal to me overall. I have more Toni Morrison books sitting on my shelf, and I most certainly won't let my slight disappointment in this one keep me from those. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13529896599924877741" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1659348238" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1659348238" />
    <a title="Rowena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena"><img alt="Rowena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499471926p2/2990642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1659348238?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rowena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rowena" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena">Rowena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330322942" href="/book/show/560209.Jazz">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2990642-rowena?shelf=african-american">african-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1659348238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7127511744667393097"><br/><br/> <b> “I’m crazy about this City. Daylight slants like a razor cutting the buildings in half. In the top half I see looking faces and it’s not easy to tell which are people, which the work of stonemasons. Below is shadow were any blasé thing takes place: clarinets and lovemaking, fists and the voices of sorrowful women. A city like this one makes me dream tall and feel in on things. Hep. It’s the bright steel rocking above the shade below that does it.”- Toni Morrison,<i> Jazz</i></b><br/><br/>Wynston Marsalis said, “Ja</span>
  <span id="freeText7127511744667393097" style="display:none"><br /><br /> <b> “I’m crazy about this City. Daylight slants like a razor cutting the buildings in half. In the top half I see looking faces and it’s not easy to tell which are people, which the work of stonemasons. Below is shadow were any blasé thing takes place: clarinets and lovemaking, fists and the voices of sorrowful women. A city like this one makes me dream tall and feel in on things. Hep. It’s the bright steel rocking above the shade below that does it.”- Toni Morrison,<i> Jazz</i></b><br /><br />Wynston Marsalis said, “Jazz is a conversation, but a nuanced, swift, and complicated one”, and over time I’ve come to learn and understand this too. What’s even more interesting to me is how the improvisation in jazz can be applied to life.<br /><br />The story starts with Violet, a woman in her 50s, mutilating the corpse of teenager Dorcas, the former lover (and murder victim) of her husband, Joe Trace. From this passionate scene at Dorcas’ funeral, we get a very emotional story which seems to be an improv, with the story lines reacting both with the city’s surroundings but also with history and personal stories.<br /><br />To me, the city backdrop and how Morrison works that into her story, is the best part of the book, in particular when the city is contrasted with the rural areas the main characters grew up in. The city carries with it its own energy and I felt it held a lot of hope and promise for people who had survived slavery and life in the countryside. Moving to the city and encountering a whole new lifestyle was a huge turning point in these people’s lives, and I like how Morrison shows that a change in scene can change everything, similar to her approach in<i> Tar Baby</i>; love is different in the city and in the countryside:<br /><br /> <b> “Little of that makes for love, but it does pump desire. The woman who churned a man’s blood as she leaned all alone on a fence by a country road might not expect even to catch his eye in the City. But if she is clipping quickly down the big-city street in heels, swinging her purse, or sitting on a stoop with a cool beer in her hand, dangling her shoe from the toes of her foot, the man, reacting to her posture, to soft skin on stone, the weight of the building stressing the delicate, dangling shoe, is captured. And he’d think it was the woman he wanted, and not some combination of curved stone, and a swinging, high-heeled shoe moving in and out of sunlight. He would know right away the deception, the trick of shapes and light and movement, but it wouldn’t matter at all because the deception was part of it too.”</b><br /><br />The first time I read this I was quite frustrated by the character of Joe Trace; male violence is always difficult to read about, and it’s even more difficult when you know the perpetrator doesn’t get the necessary punishment. Yet, and I’ve seen again and again with Morrison (and this is one of the things I admire about her the most), she is able to relay the facts in a non-judgemental way, and somehow she allows us to feel some sort of compassion.<br /><br />Apart from Dorcas, the murdered teenager, the character who I felt for most in this story is Violet. This is a lady who was clearly depressed and searching for something in life. At the age of 56 she said ,”I want some fat in this life.” This is a lady who experienced childhood tragedy, worked hard, was misunderstood, betrayed by her husband, and became the subject of gossip by her neighbours:<br /><br /> <b> “This notion of rest, it’s attractive to her, but I don’t think she would like it. They are all like that, these women. Waiting for the ease, the space that need not be filled with anything other than the drift of their own thoughts. But they wouldn’t like it. They are busy thinking of ways to be busier because such a space of nothing pressing to do would knock them down. No fields of cowslips will rush into that opening, nor mornings free of flies and heat when the light is shy. No. Not at all. They fill their minds and hands with soap and repair and dicey confrontations because what is waiting for them, in a suddenly idle moment, is the seep of rage. Molten. Thick and slow-moving. Mindful and particular about what in its path it chooses to bury.”</b><br /><br />Jazz is an emotional and a very beautiful read. Toni Morrison’s writing style is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7127511744667393097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2526359948" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2526359948" />
    <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma"><img alt="Nandakishore Varma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583648733p2/6237864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2526359948?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nandakishore Varma" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma">Nandakishore Varma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/949581.Jazz">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2526359948" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16809995388044713493"><blockquote>
  <i>Sth, I know that woman. She used to live with a flock of birds on Lenox Avenue. Know her husband, too. He fell for an eighteen-year-old girl with one of those deepdown, spooky loves that made him so sad and happy he shot her just to keep the feeling going. When the woman, her name is Violet, went to the funeral to see the funeral and cut her dead face they threw her to the floor and out of the church. She ran, then, through all that snow, and when she got back to her apartment she took the bi</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText16809995388044713493" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>Sth, I know that woman. She used to live with a flock of birds on Lenox Avenue. Know her husband, too. He fell for an eighteen-year-old girl with one of those deepdown, spooky loves that made him so sad and happy he shot her just to keep the feeling going. When the woman, her name is Violet, went to the funeral to see the funeral and cut her dead face they threw her to the floor and out of the church. She ran, then, through all that snow, and when she got back to her apartment she took the birds from their cages and set them out the windows to freeze or fly, including the parrot that said, "I love you."</i>
</blockquote><br />With this terrific first paragraph which encapsulates most of the story, Toni Morrison begins <i>Jazz</i>, her short novel which however, covers an extremely broad canvas. It is the story of Violet and Joe Trace and their waning marriage; it is the story of the puritanical Alice Manfred and her flighty niece Dorcas, who falls for Joe; it is the story of Golden Gray, the mulatto born of a black father and a white mother; it is the story of the Wild Woman, Joe Trace's mother, never seen yet always present in the woods... but above all, it is the story of Harlem in the 1920's and its sinful music: jazz.<br /><br />Morrison uses a jagged storytelling style, with the narrative shifting in the verbal equivalent of jump cuts between people, places and events. Linearity is purposefully foregone, with the author wrong-footing the reader intentionally in many places. In the middle of the novel, when we are neck-deep in the story of Violet, Joe, Dorcas and Alice, the narrative suddenly jumps to the story of Golden Gray on the quest for his father; who initially has only the most tenuous of connections to the tale, overall. The author confuses us thoroughly before tying the two threads together.<br /><br />Toni Morrison here, even while being the omnipresent narrator, confesses to being not in full charge of her characters: she says<br /><blockquote>
  <i>I ought to get out of this place. Avoid the window; leave the hole I cut through the door to get in lives instead of having one of my own. It was loving the City that distracted me and gave me ideas. Made me think I could speak its loud voice and make that sound sound human. I missed the people altogether.<br /><br />I thought I knew them and wasn't worried that they didn't really know about me. Now it's clear why they contradicted me at every turn: they knew me all along. Out of the corners of their eyes they watched me. And when I was feeling most invisible, being tight-lipped, silent and unobservable, they were whispering about me to each other. They knew how little I could be counted on; how poorly, how shabbily my know-it-all self covered helplessness. That when I invented stories about them - and doing it seemed to me so fine - I was completely in their hands, managed without mercy...</i>
</blockquote><br />The story here is writing itself, using the hapless author as a medium. To understand how its possible, one has to understand the City, and its unique music which made even unwilling people dance to its jagged and kaleidoscopic melody.<br /><br />Jazz music evolved out of the inherent need for the black people to express themselves, even when their arms, legs and even spirit were chained. Arising out of Africa's primitive music traditions, jazz was a fusion of Africa with Europe. It is non-linear and jagged; a pot-pourri of various notes and beats. Nobody would call it classical; there were many who thought it sinful; but you can't deny one thing - it makes you dance.<br /><br />Come dance, with Toni Morrison. The night is still young.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16809995388044713493" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_648784667" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/648784667" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/648784667?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/949581.Jazz">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=morrison">morrison</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer648784667" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5336033688059294312"><b>A Scandalous Trio</b><br/><br/>Jazz music is rarely the immediate subject matter of this exceptional novel. However, jazz influences much of the novel's structure and atmosphere. The narrator describes a party in terms of <i>“Red dresses. Yellow shoes. And, of course, race music to urge them on.”</i> <br/><br/>The three main protagonists – Dorcas (an 18 year old girl), Joe (a handsome 50 year old cosmetics salesman) and Violet (Joe's pretty 50 year old wife) – form an ensemble, a trio, <i>“a scandalising threesome",</i> if not exact</span>
  <span id="freeText5336033688059294312" style="display:none"><b>A Scandalous Trio</b><br /><br />Jazz music is rarely the immediate subject matter of this exceptional novel. However, jazz influences much of the novel's structure and atmosphere. The narrator describes a party in terms of <i>“Red dresses. Yellow shoes. And, of course, race music to urge them on.”</i> <br /><br />The three main protagonists – Dorcas (an 18 year old girl), Joe (a handsome 50 year old cosmetics salesman) and Violet (Joe's pretty 50 year old wife) – form an ensemble, a trio, <i>“a scandalising threesome",</i> if not exactly a menage a trois. <br /><br />The three characters are introduced, and the essence of the novel's plot is set out, in the first paragraph. In each of the following chapters, Toni Morrison delves into some of the past of each character as well as their shared past. It's as if each chapter is a solo that enables the character (or the narrator on their behalf) to improvise and elaborate on the main riff of the novel.<br /><br /><b>Crazy About This City of Jazz</b><br /><br />Jazz became a slang term for sexual intercourse soon after its creation as a musical form. It's possible that this is the main connotation of the word used in the title. The novel seems to be primarily interested in sex, lust, desire, touch, seduction, passion, romance, loneliness, longing, craving and love.<br /><br />The novel is set in Harlem in 1926. Earlier, in 1906, Joe and Violet (the descendents of black slaves) left rural Vesper County, Virginia, and moved to New York, attracted by the music and romance of the city, and the potential for better-paid jobs: <br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“Like a million more [running from want and violence] they could hardly wait to get there and love it back...<br /><br />"There, in a city, they are not so much new as themselves: their stronger, riskier selves...<br /><br />"I'm crazy about this City...<br /><br />"[It was] a City seeping music that begged and challenged each and every day. ‘Come,’ it said. ‘Come and do wrong.’...<br /><br />"It was the music. The dirty, get-on-down music the women sang and the men played and both danced to, close and shameless or apart and wild...<br /><br />"Where you can find danger or be it; where you can fight till you drop and smile at the knife when it misses and when it doesn't.”</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br /><b>Private Cracks</b><br /><br />The narrator says Violet has <i>“private cracks".</i> She suffers from a fragmented self: <br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“I call them cracks because that is what they were. Not openings or breaks, but dark fissures in the globe light of the day...<br /><br />"Sometimes when Violet isn't paying attention she stumbles into these cracks, like the time when, instead of putting her left heel forward, she stepped back and folded her legs in order to sit in the street.”</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />Joe and Violet disagree over whether to have children, and presumably are now beyond the age when it is possible or convenient. Violet stares at children in the street, and goes to bed cuddling a toy doll each night, although she and Joe aren’t obviously estranged. <i>(“He's what I got. He's what I got.”)</i> Violet explains her plight in simple terms:<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“I messed up my own life. Before I came north I made sense and so did the world. We didn't have nothing but we didn't miss it...What's the world for if you can't make it up the way you want it?”</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br /><b>To Freeze or Fly</b><br /><br />Which brings us back to the first paragraph on the first page:<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“Sth, I know that woman. She used to live with a flock of birds on Lenox Avenue. Know her husband, too. He fell for an eighteen-year-old girl with one of those deep down, spooky loves that made him so sad and happy he shot her just to keep the feeling going. When the woman, her name is Violet, went to the funeral to see the girl and to cut her dead face they threw her to the floor and out of the church. She ran, then, through all that snow, and when she got back to her apartment she took the birds from their cages and set them out the windows to freeze or fly, including the parrot that said, ‘I love you.’"</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />This language is typical of the novel. It's casual, almost conversational, yet somehow dense with information and detail. At the same time, it's both imaginative and lyrical.<br /><br /><b>Reckless, Reciprocal Love</b><br /><br />At the end of the novel, the narrator reveals her (?) own views on the quest for love:<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  <i>“I have...longed to be able to say...'that I have loved only you, surrendered my whole self reckless to you and nobody else. That I want you to love me back and show it to me. That I love the way you hold me, how close you let me be to you...’”</i>
  <br />
</blockquote><br />This focus on reciprocity seems to be a natural extension of the question of identity or the self of not just black Americans, but all people of whatever race or background, which might account for the success of Toni Morrison's novel with white readers.<br /><br />Paradoxically, this realisation occurs at the level of the narrator (an omniscient narrator who refers to herself as a <i>“know-it-all self”)</i> and the reader. It doesn't seem to emerge from the relationship between any two of the three protagonists. Indeed, it contrasts with their relationships. Perhaps, literature, art and music are the substitute for love, where it can't be found between two people. In the case of jazz, <i>“the body is the vehicle, not the point.”</i> It helps us to <i>“reach...for something beyond, way beyond and way, way down underneath tissue.”</i><br /><br /><br /><b>SOUNDTRACK:<br />["A Thorn Here, A Spike There"]</b><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><b>Terence Blanchard &amp; Branford Marsalis Quartet - "Mo' Better Blues"</b><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Q1VREP-5Rg8">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Q1VRE...</a><br /><br /><b>Gordon Webster &amp;     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_739010051" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/739010051" />
    <a title="Zanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna"><img alt="Zanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512813535p2/22591633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/739010051?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zanna" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna">Zanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/949581.Jazz">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=bechdel-pass">bechdel-pass</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer739010051" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10745820823136598997">The music happens in the background… while the folks are front and centre, every blemish inside and out on view, though modestly shaded and wrapped in gentlest understanding. Part of that understanding is history, not excavated, but unfurled or traced carefully with one finger, because it is still alive and hurting. Kinship structures the story, which curls around time, helical, branching... it is a sinewy vine, hacked at in places yet blossoming out, covering itself with fresh, lush, resurgent </span>
  <span id="freeText10745820823136598997" style="display:none">The music happens in the background… while the folks are front and centre, every blemish inside and out on view, though modestly shaded and wrapped in gentlest understanding. Part of that understanding is history, not excavated, but unfurled or traced carefully with one finger, because it is still alive and hurting. Kinship structures the story, which curls around time, helical, branching... it is a sinewy vine, hacked at in places yet blossoming out, covering itself with fresh, lush, resurgent life. A leaf is an organ. One leaf's flourishing nourishes the whole. But fallen sisters and brothers are mourned…<br /><br /><i>Where did this violence come from?</i> Joe and Violet kill and mutilate a teenage girl… and then Morrison makes us love them. Audre Lorde said "When people share a common oppression, certain kinds of skills and joint defenses are developed. And if you survive you survive because those skills and defenses have worked. When you come into conflict over other existing differences, there is a vulnerability to each other which is desperate and very deep"*. The violence of racism is digested into intraracial violence. The blood-fed and tormented vine - no wonder - bears bitter fruit.<br /><br />*Interview with Adrienne Rich, in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/730745.Sister_Outsider" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Sister Outsider</i>
</a><br /><br />One thing that struck me was the contrast between Acton and Joe. The cruel, self-centred young man fits the patriarchal expectations of Dorcas, raised by an Aunt who restricted her to protect against what she saw as a sinful youth culture. Joe, seen through his wife's eyes, is different, special, richly worthy of love, and his own telling inspires deep sympathy and liking. But it's Joe, not Acton, who destroys Dorcas, literally killing her, because it is easy, much too easy, to deal death, much too hard to reject what white supremacist capitalist patriachy teaches: that black women are expendable, that men are entitled to unconditional female loyalty.<br /><br />Missing mothers and a missing motherland for black people in America are imperfectly substituted by fellow orphan migrants to Harlem, where some kind of safety in numbers and mutual support are found. Trauma remains unarticulated, too painful for conversation, instead flowing into, being answered by the music, which flowers irrepressibly, dark blooms dripping scent and nectar, mild aphrodisiac intoxicants.<br /><br />Our narrator lives in Harlem too passing on the tales she knows, but sometimes she lets their owners tell them first or again. <i>This is how it felt to me</i> and then <i>this is how I see it</i>. The gatherer, the teller, bears an authority that comes with responsibility; she does it justice by reminding her hearers that there is no single story, only herstories and histories variously nourished and starved and intertwined.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10745820823136598997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4092502986942510039"><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597820883ra/29987159.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>Toni Morrison has been one of my favourite authors since I read one of her books (<i>Beloved</i>) for the first time. I simply cannot find any flaw in any of her books, her writing style is so rich and the understanding and portrayal of human nature she depicts in her books is beyond simple fiction. The stories of the characters of this book, described in a borderline stream of consciousness writing style; the way she sublimes the lowest, most violent and disturbing aspects of humanity in a way that i</span>
  <span id="freeText4092502986942510039" style="display:none"><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1597820883ra/29987159.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>Toni Morrison has been one of my favourite authors since I read one of her books (<i>Beloved</i>) for the first time. I simply cannot find any flaw in any of her books, her writing style is so rich and the understanding and portrayal of human nature she depicts in her books is beyond simple fiction. The stories of the characters of this book, described in a borderline stream of consciousness writing style; the way she sublimes the lowest, most violent and disturbing aspects of humanity in a way that is so unapologetic it is almost poetic; the way she creates the realest, most incredible human beings whose lives are so heartbreaking they <i>must</i> be true, makes her one of the most difficult authors to read and to stop reading. Amazing.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8115792537176125275"><i>jazz</i>. the 3rd morrison in my plan to knock ‘em all out over the next month or so…significantly weaker than the other two i’ve read, but still... it’s almost a shame that morrison writes about such incendiary and zeitgeisty stuff as you pull back much of the (mostly) nonsensical cultural criticism that surrounds her, her work, and her readers and she’s just a first class storyteller. just a great, great writer. amongst all the tragedy and despair, there’s a joyfulness in the work (and, for me, on</span>
  <span id="freeText8115792537176125275" style="display:none"><i>jazz</i>. the 3rd morrison in my plan to knock ‘em all out over the next month or so…significantly weaker than the other two i’ve read, but still... it’s almost a shame that morrison writes about such incendiary and zeitgeisty stuff as you pull back much of the (mostly) nonsensical cultural criticism that surrounds her, her work, and her readers and she’s just a first class storyteller. just a great, great writer. amongst all the tragedy and despair, there’s a joyfulness in the work (and, for me, one that overcomes the reader while digging in) that goes largely unspoken as people try and work out all the ‘important’ stuff. <br /> <br /><br />i usually don’t go for the poetic passages... but check this one from <i>Jazz</i>:<br /><br /><br /><b>It's nice when grown people whisper to each other under the covers. Their ecstasy is more leaf-sigh than bray and the body is the vehicle, not the point. They reach, grown people, for something beyond, way beyond and way, way down underneath tissue. They are remembering while they whisper the carnival dolls they won and the Baltimore boats they never sailed on. …Breathing and murmuring under covers both of them have washed and hung out on the line, in a bed they chose together and kept together nevermind one leg was propped on a 1916 dictionary, and the mattress, curved like a preacher's palm asking for witnesses in His name's sake, enclosed them each and every night and muffled their whispering, old-time love. They are under the covers because they don't have to look at themselves anymore.</b><br /><br /><br />In re-reading the above, two other passages come to mind. the first from martin amis and the second from the greatest poet of the last century (that's right!), philip larkin. If you’re interested... <br /><br /><br /><b>Cities at night, I feel, contain men who cry in their sleep and then say Nothing. It’s nothing. Just sad dreams. Or something like that… Swing low in your weep ship, with your tear scans and your sob probes, and you would mark them. Women – and they can be wives, lovers, gaunt muses, fat nurses, obsessions, devourers, exes, nemeses – will wake and turn to these men and ask, with female need-to-know, “What is it?” And the men say, “Nothing. No it isn’t anything really. Just sad dreams."</b><br /><br /><br />first paragraph from <i>the information</i>. <br /><br />and:<br /><br /><b>What do they think has happened, the old fools,<br />To make them like this? Do they somehow suppose<br />It's more grown-up when your mouth hangs open and drools,<br />And you keep on pissing yourself, and can't remember<br />Who called this morning? Or that, if they only chose,<br />They could alter things back to when they danced all night,<br />Or went to their wedding, or sloped arms some September?<br />Or do they fancy there's really been no change,<br />And they've always behaved as if they were crippled or tight,<br />Or sat through days of thin continuous dreaming<br />Watching light move? If they don't (and they can't), it's strange:<br />Why aren't they screaming?</b><br /><br /> first stanza of <i>the old fools</i><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_2762249831" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2762249831" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2336594136624377830">Incredible! Lyrical and sublime! Ms. Morrison portrays the post slavery period in America (just after the Civil War) and into the 20th century as well as any writer I have read who has had the courage to deal with this period, a dark period in American history where people of color might have been free, but not really. Her characters are unforgettable and so real and her writing transcends the time and place of her writing and its brilliance is everlasting. AMAZING!!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_151217053" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151217053" />
    <a title="Ems Dawson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5053950-ems-dawson"><img alt="Ems Dawson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299106050p2/5053950.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/151217053?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ems Dawson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ems Dawson" href="/user/show/5053950-ems-dawson">Ems Dawson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151217053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14901036990557039718">One of my favorite books of all time!<br/><br/>I was lucky enough to study this book during 6th form college with a good teacher. Instead of butchering its beauty she illuminated it; leading us through the more complex prose (their beauty all more appreciated due to a deeper level of understanding) and highlighting some of the more obscure elements that might have gone unnoticed (or perhaps not understood).<br/><br/>At 16, though not niave, I was perhaps unaware of the many elements and angles of understanding rela</span>
  <span id="freeText14901036990557039718" style="display:none">One of my favorite books of all time!<br /><br />I was lucky enough to study this book during 6th form college with a good teacher. Instead of butchering its beauty she illuminated it; leading us through the more complex prose (their beauty all more appreciated due to a deeper level of understanding) and highlighting some of the more obscure elements that might have gone unnoticed (or perhaps not understood).<br /><br />At 16, though not niave, I was perhaps unaware of the many elements and angles of understanding related to racism, especially in America (which seemed a world far removed). But there is much more to Morrison's Jazz than American prose. Unlike so many others, that parade the usual melting pot, American Dream, Racism themes, Morrison examines human relationships in a real and down to earth way.<br /><br />Having finished the book I walked around in a daze for a couple of days reconsidering almost everything I had previously thought (that is no overstatement). Though some obvious questions are raised (especially the lives of african-Americans), I did not meditate on racism or poverty, but rather relationships and the ties between human beings.<br /><br />I'm not sure if it was because of the time of my life that i read it, or whatever, but to me Jazz spoke volumes.<br /><br />This book really is amazing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14901036990557039718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2966647030" />
    <a title="♥ Sandi ❣	" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4984609-sandi"><img alt="♥ Sandi ❣	" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597618544p2/4984609.jpg" /></a>

  <span id="freeText881954958112963355" style="display:none">3 stars<br /><br />I just have to admit that I am not really a Toni Morrison fan. I have read a couple of her books and like this one, they just did not make a lot of sense to me. It is not the eclectic conversations in her books, I just feel that her story lines are scattered and I have a terrible time following her. If I think I am following her thoughts, I eventually end up at a dead stop, wondering where things are going or what I just read and the purpose of it. <br /><br />I gave this book a 3 star rating not because I liked the book, but because she was such an honored and well liked author who has won numerous awards for her writing. Just because I do not care for her writing does not mean that she was not accomplished. Sadly, I just do not connect with her. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5648376974375635507">I liked this more the first time I read it. Though the actual writing is pretty faultless, as is often the case with any of <br/>Mother Toni’s novels, for me there is an issue of too much style, too much substance. I almost wish the novel was double its size; the themes would then have more room to develop and connect, which they rather tenuously do in the novel as written. I do love the very free-form, improvisatory style, the total lack of traditional narrative sequence, but the dramatic engine of </span>
  <span id="freeText5648376974375635507" style="display:none">I liked this more the first time I read it. Though the actual writing is pretty faultless, as is often the case with any of <br />Mother Toni’s novels, for me there is an issue of too much style, too much substance. I almost wish the novel was double its size; the themes would then have more room to develop and connect, which they rather tenuously do in the novel as written. I do love the very free-form, improvisatory style, the total lack of traditional narrative sequence, but the dramatic engine of Jazz relies almost exclusively on symbolism and metaphor, much of which is unclear due to its obfuscatory lyricism. That said...Toni is a genius and the Queen of my literary life, now and forever.</span>

<div id="review_438464513" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/438464513" />
    <a title="Oscar Calva" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11395996-oscar-calva"><img alt="Oscar Calva" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347431062p2/11395996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/438464513?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oscar Calva" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oscar Calva" href="/user/show/11395996-oscar-calva">Oscar Calva</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400076215" href="/book/show/11341.Jazz">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11395996-oscar-calva?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11395996-oscar-calva?shelf=nobel-author">nobel-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer438464513" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4847356262060949085">Before my review, some random thoughts on some jazz albums in my collection. I love sixties jazz and I'm undecided if <i>Blue Train</i> or <i>A Love Supreme</i> is my favorite Coltrane album (he's definitely my favorite jazz musician), and I'm not going to enter the tired discussion if the latter or Miles' <i>Kind of Blue</i> is the greatest jazz album ever recorded. However there are two very dear gems in my collection that usually are not mentioned when discussing the top 10 albums of all time. One of them <i>Monk's </i></span>
  <span id="freeText4847356262060949085" style="display:none">Before my review, some random thoughts on some jazz albums in my collection. I love sixties jazz and I'm undecided if <i>Blue Train</i> or <i>A Love Supreme</i> is my favorite Coltrane album (he's definitely my favorite jazz musician), and I'm not going to enter the tired discussion if the latter or Miles' <i>Kind of Blue</i> is the greatest jazz album ever recorded. However there are two very dear gems in my collection that usually are not mentioned when discussing the top 10 albums of all time. One of them <i>Monk's Dream</i> (Thelonius Monk), the other <i>The Köln Concert</i> (Keith Jarrett), and I think those two albums are perfect introductions to what to expect reading Toni Morrison's <i>Jazz</i><br /><br />Neither, even though very important works, might be considered quintessential jazz albums; compared to other albums in his catalog, <i>Monk's Dream</i> doesn't sound as progressive or brilliantly awkward, it's not even original as most of the tracks here had been previously released elsewhere. Nevertheless it's still very tight and effortless complex --key word: effortless--, as if having that band playing for years those tracks and subconsciously knowing each note, rythm and movement in perfect synchronicity but still sounding like everything was improvised on one take, and as complex as some moments result, the overall music experience is very enjoyable and comfortable to the listener. On the other hand, <i>"The Köln Concert"</i> is a very gorgeous, trippy and hypnotizing live album, elegant without any pretentiousness, Jarrett sounds as if rehearsing poem writing with his piano while spreading hallucinogens on the air.<br /><br />Similarly Morrison's <i>Jazz</i> is not her magnum opus, far from it, but the cadence, lyricism and beauty of her written words is very Jarrett-like, at the same time her writing is, while still technically very complex, as effortless complex as Monk's. She writes as if words just write themselves and none is out of place even if the reader at times has trouble grasping her continuous "free jazz" long streams of writing; the flow of the words has the same effect as listening to "Bright Mississipi" (my favorite track in Monk's Dream). Here a sample:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>And when spring comes to the City people notice one another in the road; notice the strangers with whom they share aisles and tables and the space where intimate garments are laundered. Going in and out, in and out the same door, they handle the handle; on trolleys and park benches they settle thighs on a seat in which hundreds have done it too. Copper coins dropped in the palm have been swallowed by children and tested by gypsies, but it’s still money and people smile at that. It’s the time of year when the City urges contradiction most, encouraging you to buy street food when you have no appetite at all; giving you a taste for a single room occupied by you alone as well as a craving to share it with someone you passed in the street. Really there is no contradiction—rather it’s a condition: the range of what an artful City can do. What can beat bricks warming up to the sun? The return of awnings. The removal of blankets from horses’ backs. Tar softens under the heel and the darkness under bridges changes from gloom to cooling shade. After a light rain, when the leaves have come, tree limbs are like wet fingers playing in woolly green hair. Motor cars become black jet boxes gliding behind hoodlights weakened by mist. On sidewalks turned to satin figures move shoulder first, the crowns of their heads angled shields against the light buckshot that the raindrops are.</i>
</blockquote> <br /> <br />Yup, <i>Jazz</i> is definitely a very musical book, and Toni Morrison is as good at "playing music" with a pen as much as Coltrane is to "writing epic novels" with a tenor sax, but this is neither her <i>A Love Supreme</i> or her <i>Kind of Blue</i>, the reason I don't give it 5 stars is because it still is a very uneven composition.<br /><br />As for the plot/story, there are a lot of reviews out there which make a better summary, and in this novel the plot and the story are very irrelevant compared to the beautiful writing. Enough to say, this is a story of a passion between an old man and a young woman ending in tragedy, which, being an immigrant african american in the Harlem Renaissance era has also an historic imprint of past tragedies.<br /><br />But it's better explained by the author herself in a single quote:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Whatever happens, whether you get rich or stay poor, ruin your health or live to old age, you always end up back where you started: hungry for the one thing everybody loses - young loving.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="4847356262060949085" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_189278596" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/189278596" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875291-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565352267p2/5875291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/189278596?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/5875291-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400076215" href="/book/show/11341.Jazz">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875291-tim?shelf=african-studies">african-studies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer189278596" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4599798039817948048">Anyone who has been through adversity knows the view. It’s that view of life stripped down to nothing but the basic. All you’re left with is your breath, and sometimes that feels like it’s slipping away. But you still have something, even if it’s ugly, even if it has no map, even if no one cares. What happens next is a choice. You can choose to take the basics of life that are left and build around them. What you weave becomes something on your terms. Why else does adversity create some of the b</span>
  <span id="freeText4599798039817948048" style="display:none">Anyone who has been through adversity knows the view. It’s that view of life stripped down to nothing but the basic. All you’re left with is your breath, and sometimes that feels like it’s slipping away. But you still have something, even if it’s ugly, even if it has no map, even if no one cares. What happens next is a choice. You can choose to take the basics of life that are left and build around them. What you weave becomes something on your terms. Why else does adversity create some of the best art? It sheds that which arrived from the outside.<br /><br />On a large scale, adversity is represented in oppression. Old paradigms no longer apply. Either something new is created, or death occurs. Jazz, because life requires improvisation; this is a route that no one has traveled. People constantly thrown into immobile structures have to re-create themselves to absorb the impact. Adaptation regains control.<br /><br />Understanding WHY is not always the intention. A Toni Morrison novel is beautiful through the way it weaves in and around. You ride on the words, seeing areas that compliment rather than obscure. Stay on the surface, don’t drown. Like the voice guiding us through “Jazz”, life calls for your touch. In a society where imitation is expected, individuality becomes destroyed.<br /><br />Your heritage merges with the present, and becomes the future. But the route is quickly adjusted to accommodate the other. Recognize the part. Move, bend, and retain your tone. Someone else is not playing your role, but you do live among them. Hate stops the music. To keep the interplay vibrant and tonal, we love.<br /><br />Open up and learn how to live. Music is able to be enjoyed because one note leads to the next. Why move so quickly? Slow down and listen. Once it’s over, that time and place are gone.<br /><br />Morrison confounds in a world where we don’t know how to experience. Just be. Soak everything in. The journey is seen through your lens. Let all you are and all you’ve ever been become your expression. Then learn to see through the lens of the suffering. Bring more and more artistry to the piece by allowing their voices to bend around your own. Beautiful harmonies are then created.<br /><br />Sometimes dissonance will occur. Like jazz, life is improvised as you go. The exact same note-pattern-tone never occurs twice. Learn from that. Move with it. Look for the best. Make the tone return to a rich, sweet sound.<br /><br />Don’t let others put restrictions on your artistry. Never conform. We are here to complete, not to take away that soul, that deep passion from our fellow musicians.<br /><br />Morrison comes from a people who have mastered this art. However, we share life, and improvisation is required from everyone to make a unified expression. We all have our own forms of adversity. Use it to learn how to express. Take the experience, and don’t regret. Move forward never back. What you played in the past is over. The future is always a new piece. But the musical proficiency is easier as you go, and the fun part, like reading Morrison, is experiencing each note in that one moment it’s played. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4599798039817948048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2228740297" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2228740297" />
    <a title="Robert Case" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24463210-robert-case"><img alt="Robert Case" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494969301p2/24463210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2228740297?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Case" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Case" href="/user/show/24463210-robert-case">Robert Case</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24463210-robert-case?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24463210-robert-case?shelf=literature-american">literature-american</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2228740297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7936607554819155317">Jazz is a historical novel told in many voices. It is set in Harlem during the Jazz Age and tells the story of a long term relationship between a husband and wife that is in deep trouble. In lyrical prose the reader learns that the husband's young lover is already dead. Violet, his wife narrates. She explains how indifferent they are towards each other, her love for the City, her disdain for the dead woman, and how it was that she came to disfigure the young woman's corpse with a knife at the fu</span>
  <span id="freeText7936607554819155317" style="display:none">Jazz is a historical novel told in many voices. It is set in Harlem during the Jazz Age and tells the story of a long term relationship between a husband and wife that is in deep trouble. In lyrical prose the reader learns that the husband's young lover is already dead. Violet, his wife narrates. She explains how indifferent they are towards each other, her love for the City, her disdain for the dead woman, and how it was that she came to disfigure the young woman's corpse with a knife at the funeral. <br />All this information arrives in the first chapter, grisly facts upon which to hook a reader. But somehow, it works. By the end of the second chapter, a flashback to hard times in rural Virginia and a young couple train-dancing into New York City, the young marrieds, Joe and Violet, have become fascinating and sympathetic characters. Their stories are a composite of artful storytelling.<br />The plot follows the evolution of their life-long relationship. The result is a compelling look at love, poverty, and commitment in the USA during the turning points of the 19th and 20th centuries.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7936607554819155317" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2179494163" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2179494163" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2179494163?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2179494163" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14591709013913287906">An unmistakable musical cadence echoes through the voices of the various narrators of "Jazz"; the ebb and flow of the narration reverberating with the tempo and intonation of the inner lives of the characters, from the violent romanticism of Joe, the vituperation of Violet or the hopeful lyricism of Dorcas, Morrison imbues the characters with their own inner voice, as each of them tells the story of Joe Crace and his affair with Dorcas and his eventual murder of her.<br/><br/>Morrison explores the post-sl</span>
  <span id="freeText14591709013913287906" style="display:none">An unmistakable musical cadence echoes through the voices of the various narrators of "Jazz"; the ebb and flow of the narration reverberating with the tempo and intonation of the inner lives of the characters, from the violent romanticism of Joe, the vituperation of Violet or the hopeful lyricism of Dorcas, Morrison imbues the characters with their own inner voice, as each of them tells the story of Joe Crace and his affair with Dorcas and his eventual murder of her.<br /><br />Morrison explores the post-slavery shift in the African-American experience, from the initial freedom of breaking free from the chains of slavery, to the realisation that they had become entrapped in a more insidious form or enslavement, where the chains African-Americans were forced to wear were no longer physical, but tacit and the febrile freedom offered by cities to African-Americans to enable them to overcome the prejudices they faced, but how this caused them to forget the beauty of the country in comparison to the artificiality of the cities;<br /><br />"He forgets a sun that used to slide up like the egg yolk of a good country egg, thick and red-orange at the bottom of the sky, and he doesn't miss it, doesn't look up to see what happened to it or the stars made irrelevant  by the light of thrilling, wasteful lamps."<br /><br />Yet, beyond all this, Jazz is a wonderfully told account of the inner lives of the characters caught up in the romance between Joe and Dorcas. Joe, charming and convivial, is caugh up in a passion for Dorcas whilst visiting her house; Joe references that he had never tasted sweetness until he tasted the honey of Dorcas's skin, yet it is honey which is tainted with poison and which drives Joe to jealously murder Dorcas, who, being 32 years younger than Joe, feels suffocated by his sentimentality and begins seeing a somewhat brutish character named Acton. Observing all this is Joe's half-mad wife Violet. The reader is left constantly uncertain about how or what to believe, as each of the narrators is unintentionally unreliable, each wrapped up in their own hopes, dreams and fears beneath the violence and vituperation of 1920's Harlem in which the vast majority of the novel is set.<br /><br /><br />The novel is frequently broken apart and fragmented beneath the different narrative voices, and becomes increasingly fragmented, yet febrile, rich with the inner lives of the characters, whose journeys to find a sense of identity, whether it be Joe' search for his parents or Violet's such for love, is central to the emotional journeys they go through. 'Jazz' is one of Morrison's masterpieces. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14591709013913287906" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1311956539" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1311956539" />
    <a title="Daniel Clausen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/957634-daniel-clausen"><img alt="Daniel Clausen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603533755p2/957634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1311956539?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Clausen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Clausen" href="/user/show/957634-daniel-clausen">Daniel Clausen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1311956539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2323517151674256212"><br/>It’s a mature book in every way. Perhaps older and less ambitious than Beloved or other of Toni Morrison’s books. The maturity really shines in the later chapters where we get to know the narrator and the relationship between Joe and Violent in more depth. We don’t need an explosive end, only to know that life moves on in rhythms and rhymes we -- as authors and readers -- don’t completely understand. <br/><br/>I was convinced that the narrator was another personality of Violet -- that the narrator lived i</span>
  <span id="freeText2323517151674256212" style="display:none"><br />It’s a mature book in every way. Perhaps older and less ambitious than Beloved or other of Toni Morrison’s books. The maturity really shines in the later chapters where we get to know the narrator and the relationship between Joe and Violent in more depth. We don’t need an explosive end, only to know that life moves on in rhythms and rhymes we -- as authors and readers -- don’t completely understand. <br /><br />I was convinced that the narrator was another personality of Violet -- that the narrator lived in That other Violet who was crazy and out of control. But as I read the last chapter again, I realized that Violet was Toni Morrison’s stand in. I also realized that she wasn’t. Violet, like all of the characters in the book, evolved and reinvented herself in ways Morrison couldn’t predict. They had to. That was the only way to survive The City. To survive at all in that time and place. <br /><br />How did the characters do this? Because they are alive! Every author begins to feel this way about their characters -- after a while. It takes maturity to let your characters walk on their own by and by and to not try to make them into something other than what they are. <br /><br />And Joe and Violet? They didn’t want to be climactic characters -- they had their own designs, for an adulthood happier than most, somewhat romantic, odd in its way, but as human as anything else that has walked on this earth. <br /><br />How do you reach that point -- that point where the music takes over and everything that you wanted to control controls itself in ways that make you but a vessel? The narrator doesn’t quite know. I don’t quite know. And that’s what makes good fiction magical. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2323517151674256212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1015783465">
  <td colspan="2">

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_1015783465'><a id="like_count_review_1015783465" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1015783465?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">12 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F37398.Jazz">Like</a></span>
