    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1067255880" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1067255880" />
    <a title="Momina Masood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17262906-momina-masood"><img alt="Momina Masood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1423900868p2/17262906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1067255880?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Momina Masood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Momina Masood" href="/user/show/17262906-momina-masood">Momina Masood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17262906-momina-masood?shelf=african-american-lit">african-american-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17262906-momina-masood?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17262906-momina-masood?shelf=500-must-reads">500-must-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1067255880" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer607066950740037712"><blockquote>
  <i>“We born around the same time, me and you,” said Violet. “We women, me and you. Tell me something real. Don’t just say I’m grown and ought to know. I don’t. I’m fifty and I don’t know nothing. What about it? Do I stay with him? I want to, I think. I want... well, I didn't always... now I want. I want some fat in this life.” <br/><br/>“Wake up. Fat or lean, you got just one. This is it.” <br/><br/>“You don’t know either, do you?” <br/><br/>“I know enough to know how to behave.” <br/><br/>“Is that it? Is that all it is?”</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>Perhaps, som</span>
  <span id="freeText607066950740037712" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“We born around the same time, me and you,” said Violet. “We women, me and you. Tell me something real. Don’t just say I’m grown and ought to know. I don’t. I’m fifty and I don’t know nothing. What about it? Do I stay with him? I want to, I think. I want... well, I didn't always... now I want. I want some fat in this life.” <br /><br />“Wake up. Fat or lean, you got just one. This is it.” <br /><br />“You don’t know either, do you?” <br /><br />“I know enough to know how to behave.” <br /><br />“Is that it? Is that all it is?”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Perhaps, someone can help me with this, but despite all the talk of “change” and looking ahead and not allowing the past to entrap us in her preface, I see Morrison actually preoccupied with its reconstruction, rather than allowing her characters to escape its confines, its memory. Memory is what defines this novel, both structurally and thematically. In this respect, <i>Song of Solomon</i>, though regressive in its own way, still moves ahead. The narrative sails linearly from Milkman’s childhood to adolescence though in a way it, too, is preoccupied with the reconstruction of Milkman’s ancestral history, the past, its memory. But the narrative still moves ahead. <i>Jazz</i>, on the other head, begins from the present and moves all the way back; there is no plot to speak of, everything worth knowing in terms of “story” is mentioned in the first chapter. What follows is what actually came first. There is Joe Trace, haunted by the mother he never knew, remembering his attempts to “trace” his origins, and finding them again in the youthful cheeks of his lover, Dorcas. (The treatment of the female body is so freaking interesting in Morrison, I’ll get to that in a minute.) Then there is Violet, his wife, an unlicensed beautician, childless, haunted by her grandmother’s stories of a golden-haired boy, a mulatto, a prince, a beautiful creature who in turn sought his own origins. Orphans abound in this novel, and it’s up to the reader if he or she decides to be ambitious enough to extend this "orphanhood" and read it as the existential condition of the African American of the '20s. Was Morrison anti-integration? If not, and the question itself is preposterous, why then do her characters always find themselves returning to the past, looking for their origins, haunted by the memory of something glorious they were once a part of but which they can't remember? Milkman ends his journey in the middle of a wilderness, and over the dead body of Pilate realizes finally that he, too, can fly! Zora's Janie finds in the Everglades her true self—can’t the ambitious reader, then, interpret these swamps and wildernesses as the memory of Africa, the longing to return “home”, the search for the metaphorical “mother” they were brutally cut off from? I mean, Joe’s mother is named “Wild” here in the novel, so I don’t know how it could be any more obvious. :P<br /><br />But as I lack any concrete knowledge of Morrison’s personal views, I realize it's crazy to jump to conclusions. The same evidence could be construed in a different way, of course, and this is what I love about the reading process. Anyway, coming to the female body—Pilate in <i>Song of Solomon</i> lacks a navel, and it is she who in fact eases Milkman’s journey to his origins. In <i>Jazz</i>, the “hoof marks” in Dorcas’ cheeks reminds Joe of his mother and his attempts at finding her. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“Little half moons clustered underneath her cheekbones, like faint hoofmarks. There and on her forehead. I bought the stuff she told me to, but glad none of it ever worked. Take my little hoof marks away? Leave me with no tracks at all? In this world the best thing, the only thing, is to find the trail and stick to it.”</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />The next immediate sentence begins the retelling of Joe’s futile endeavors at finding his mother who, as local legend would have it, was a madwoman insistent on living in the forests, having abandoned little Joe when he was born. Named “Wild” by the locality because of obvious reasons, she becomes the stable “point in all this immensity” for Joe, without whom he remains forever lost and bewildered. Morrison’s novels are, hence, feministic in their symbolism of the female body, and how closely she associates her female characters to the search for self-actualization. “Trees”, “roots”, “leaves”—all these metaphors are associated with Morrison’s women who might not appear to be overtly dynamic, but still take their men by the hand to a place where all might be well, where everyone “knows their name”.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"The third time Joe had tried to find her (he was a married man by then) he had searched the hillside for the tree—the one whose roots grew backward as though, having gone obediently into earth and found it barren, retreating to the trunk for what was needed. Defiant and against logic its roots climbed. Toward leaves, light, wind."</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Her feminism is of a different sort than Zora's—women in Morrison are the bearers of tradition and ritual, metaphors for existential grounding and fulfillment. And I think that’s absolutely brilliant because it's so subtle.<br /><br />Structurally, unlike <i>Song of Solomon</i>, the novel is rather experimental and challenging. The narrative jumps back and forth from the present to the past tense, sometimes to the present-continuous as Morrison insists on “showing” rather than “describing”; the reader, thus, becomes an invisible observer midst the story as it unfolds around him. We are there. We see it happen. She has employed different POVs, and we're alternately taken into the minds of her various characters who <s>tell</s> remember their story, and we remember it with them. So, you have at times the omniscient narrator from up above, telling it how he/she sees it, and sometimes you have flashbacks written in a rapid stream-of-consciousness style when a character takes over. And it all flows like jazz music—Morrison conducts her orchestra like a maestro with no narrative voice sounding cacophonous and out of place, rather perfectly in tune with all the others. <br /><br />If not as compelling and poetic as <i>Song of Solomon</i>, Morrison’s fans will still appreciate this novel as it talks of love, and how sometimes all we need is a little grace and acceptance to get through life.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="607066950740037712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2316518944" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2316518944" />
    <a title="Tanya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya"><img alt="Tanya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602837639p2/15698555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2316518944?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanya" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya">Tanya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/30137761-jazz">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2316518944" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer681488164106267367">A plot synopsis can't possibly do the complexity of this book justice, but I will quote the opening paragraph, which gives a good enough idea and hooked me from the first page:<br/><br/><blockquote><i>"Sth, I know that woman. She used to live with a flock of birds on Lenox Avenue. Know her husband, too. He fell for an eighteen-year-old girl with one of those deepdown, spooky loves that made him so sad and happy he shot her just to keep the feeling going. When the woman, her name is Violet, went to the funeral to see the</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText681488164106267367" style="display:none">A plot synopsis can't possibly do the complexity of this book justice, but I will quote the opening paragraph, which gives a good enough idea and hooked me from the first page:<br /><br /><blockquote><i>"Sth, I know that woman. She used to live with a flock of birds on Lenox Avenue. Know her husband, too. He fell for an eighteen-year-old girl with one of those deepdown, spooky loves that made him so sad and happy he shot her just to keep the feeling going. When the woman, her name is Violet, went to the funeral to see the funeral and cut her dead face they threw her to the floor and out of the church. She ran, then, through all that snow, and when she got back to her apartment she took the birds from their cages and set them out the windows to freeze or fly, including the parrot that said,</i> "I love you.""</blockquote><br /><br />The second in Morrison's Dantesque trilogy on African-American history, which began in Hell with the Pulitzer Prize winning <i>Beloved</i>, continues in Purgatory with <i>Jazz</i>, mostly set in Harlem in the 1920's, although the narrative eventually extends back to the American South some seventy years before through jagged flashbacks and jump-cut reminiscences. With a host of shifting, unreliable narrators (the author herself becomes one), which are often not clearly identifiable, this is a rather challenging read, as Morrison renounces linearity to befuddle the reader; in this way, the novel is uneven and disjointed, and mirrors the music that gives it its name, which in turn evolved from the undeniable need of African Americans to express themselves even when they were shackled in body and spirit.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"...the past was an abused record with no choice but to repeat itself at the crack and no power on earth could lift the arm that held the needle."</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />I admit that I often got lost, but I loved wandering in her lyricism and symbolism, and finding my muddled tracks again. Each character's "composition"—confusing, seemingly unrelated, and often vastly different in tone; like single, spontaneous instruments in a Jazz ensemble—eventually fit together to assemble a single cohesive story of love and obsession from the hopes, fears and harsh realities of African-Americans who've left behind their rural past for the promise of an urban future.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"I’m crazy about this City. Daylight slants like a razor cutting the buildings in half. In the top half I see looking faces and it’s not easy to tell which are people, which the work of stonemasons. Below is shadow where any blasé thing takes place: clarinets and lovemaking, fists and the voices of sorrowful women. A city like this one makes me dream tall and feel in on things. Hep. It’s the bright steel rocking above the shade below that does it. When I look over strips of green grass lining the river, at church steeples and into the cream-and-copper halls of apartment buildings, I’m strong. Alone, yes, but top-notch and indestructible—like the City in 1926 when all the wars are over and there will never be another one. The people down there in the shadow are happy about that. At last, at last, everything’s ahead. The smart ones say so and people listening to them and reading what they write down agree: Here comes the new. Look out."</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Every paragraph is full of stunning poetic import; words both written and left unsaid so laden with pain and wonder that it becomes poetry. Morrison turns her skillful hand at themes that so many other writers fail at illuminating with such an authentic touch: Her works aren't just about slavery and racism and pursuing the American Dream, but about real human relationships anyone can relate to, no matter their background.<br /><br />—————<br /><br />My other reviews of Morrison's Dantesque trilogy:<br /><br />01: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1906756174" rel="nofollow noopener">Beloved</a> · ★★★★★<br />02: Jazz · ★★★★<br />03: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2316518970" rel="nofollow noopener">Paradise</a> · ★★★★★</span>
  <a data-text-id="681488164106267367" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_482954156" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/482954156" />
    <a title="Holly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9131791-holly"><img alt="Holly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356101265p2/9131791.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/482954156?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Holly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Holly" href="/user/show/9131791-holly">Holly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9131791-holly?shelf=women-s-novels">women-s-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer482954156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9091766225479415768">SPOILERS<br/><br/>Overview: Joe Trace is a middle aged married man (Violet, wife) who has an affair with an 18 year old Dorkas and shoots her over anguish of her breaking up with him. <br/><br/>Characters: The first half of the book looked at Violet and touched on the affair and general growing up aspects in what I would call a simple to read type writing. However, the second half of the book was almost written by a different author. I actually had to read 10% of the book again because I had difficulty understandin</span>
  <span id="freeText9091766225479415768" style="display:none">SPOILERS<br /><br />Overview: Joe Trace is a middle aged married man (Violet, wife) who has an affair with an 18 year old Dorkas and shoots her over anguish of her breaking up with him. <br /><br />Characters: The first half of the book looked at Violet and touched on the affair and general growing up aspects in what I would call a simple to read type writing. However, the second half of the book was almost written by a different author. I actually had to read 10% of the book again because I had difficulty understanding Truebell and GoldenGrey's role in the book. The second half focused more on Joe but veered off into Joe's family tree vs. concentrating on Joe himself. <br /><br />What was missing: I didn't care. While there was history on the characters from their birth to present day I just didn't care. I didn't care why he cheated - why Violet let the birds go - why Dorkas let herself die. I wasn't drawn into the book. I could picture the scenery and the conversations but just didn't care. <br /><br />Would I read it again - No. <br />Would I recommend it - No.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1388247067?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=rth-lifetime">rth-lifetime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2015">2015</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1388247067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12194554735332846666">Toni Morrison wrote a trilogy exploring African-American history through three crucial periods. The brilliant <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/718679822' title='My review' rel='nofollow noopener'>Beloved</a> was in slavery times. The third book, Paradise, I don't know about - 50s? - I haven't read it yet. And Jazz tackles the Harlem Renaissance, which is very exciting to me because that was an awesome time and place.<br/><br/>My issue is that it doesn't actually spend very much time in Harlem in the 20s. Morrison is interested in the Great Migration - the process by which all these black peopl</span>
  <span id="freeText12194554735332846666" style="display:none">Toni Morrison wrote a trilogy exploring African-American history through three crucial periods. The brilliant <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/718679822" title="My review" rel="nofollow noopener">Beloved</a> was in slavery times. The third book, Paradise, I don't know about - 50s? - I haven't read it yet. And Jazz tackles the Harlem Renaissance, which is very exciting to me because that was an awesome time and place.<br /><br />My issue is that it doesn't actually spend very much time in Harlem in the 20s. Morrison is interested in the Great Migration - the process by which all these black people ended up in Harlem in the first place - and it all feels a little anthropological to me. I liked the central story, in which a guy kills his mistress and then the guy's wife shows up to disrupt the mistress's funeral. But I kept getting pulled away from it so everyone could tell their origin stories. <br /><br />Morrison has two bad habits: postmodernism and corniness. They're both over-indulged in here, and this isn't my favorite Morrison.</span>

<div id="review_3409495376" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3409495376" />
    <a title="Rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1083872-rachel"><img alt="Rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1208222543p2/1083872.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3409495376?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel" href="/user/show/1083872-rachel">Rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307388100" href="/book/show/7022686-jazz">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1083872-rachel?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3409495376" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2064311687174111888">Man, how does an author even follow up on the widely acclaimed modern classic that is BELOVED? By writing about Jazz!<br/><br/>This is still historical fiction, but we’re back in the 20th century, for the most part. Particularly Harlem, in the years after the first world war. The main story revolves around a young girl, Dorcas, who was killed by her older lover, Joe. But far from the drama ending there, Joe’s wife, Violet, accosts the body at the funeral (and gets herself the nickname “Violent.”)<br/><br/>What is t</span>
  <span id="freeText2064311687174111888" style="display:none">Man, how does an author even follow up on the widely acclaimed modern classic that is BELOVED? By writing about Jazz!<br /><br />This is still historical fiction, but we’re back in the 20th century, for the most part. Particularly Harlem, in the years after the first world war. The main story revolves around a young girl, Dorcas, who was killed by her older lover, Joe. But far from the drama ending there, Joe’s wife, Violet, accosts the body at the funeral (and gets herself the nickname “Violent.”)<br /><br />What is the point to all off this madness? Is it all to be laid at the feet, like Dorcas’s aunt thinks it should be, at the sinuous influence of jazz music? Meh, I don’t think so. Violence is everywhere—institutional white violence against Blacks, and then inner community crime. Including in country settings, and of course going right back to the days of slavery. No Morrison novel is just a ripped from the headlines tabloid piece. She wants to dive into the flowing rivers of history.<br /><br />There’s a lot of characters at play, and with Morrison’s stream of consciousness style they can be hard to keep track of. What stood out to me, as always, was Morrison’s commentary on colorism and racial identity. Dorcas was light skinned, so desirable, while Violet was darker. Violet grew up with a story from her grandmother, who was born in slavery, of the young mistress in her household getting impregnated by a slave. She flees her father’s wrath, from Virginia to Baltimore, but ends up not getting rid of the baby. The baby is so light skinned that they call him Golden Gray. When he finally learns the truth of his lineage, he goes to confront his father out of fear and rage. There, he inadvertently steps into Joe’s backstory, where his mother, a wild woman, is giving birth and running off.<br /><br />A lot of these are the internal realities of Black people. Dorcas’s realities are much more external. Both of her parents were killed in white riots. Her strict aunt brought her up, but much like Joe, it seems Dorcas is looking for a replacement figure. I struggled a bit with Dorcas’s death. How much the other characters, both directly and indirectly responsible for it, seemed to dismiss her. She was their <i>victim,</i> and she didn’t deserve to die! Then again, who in this novel deserved their treatment? At least in the case of Dorcas, at least in the case of the Black people, they come together—Violet and Dorcas’s aunt, Violet, Joe and Dorcas’s friend Felice—to try and make sense of their lives. To reach out to each other, even. To express remorse. It’s all so much more human than the institutional violence from outside the community.<br /><br />And in between all of this is the music and rhythm of Jazz. And sometimes of other music, too. As Toni Morrison describes in her foreword: “Like the music that came to be known as Jazz, she took from everywhere, knew everything—gospel, classic, blues, hymns—and made it her own.”<br /><br />There is a narrator to this story. Like in many of Morrison’s works, sometimes the characters speak for themselves in first person. But there’s another first person, too. Who is it, this voice that sometimes seems to imply that the other characters are “manifestation of the music’s intellect, sensuality, anarchy”? I’m quoting from Morrison’s foreword again, and I think the answer is Jazz. She wrote a book to be the novelization of a storied, musical tradition. Talk about an encore to BELOVED. That’s so freaking cool.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2064311687174111888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1876436560" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1876436560" />
    <a title="Joni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5000486-joni"><img alt="Joni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584797095p2/5000486.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1876436560?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joni" href="/user/show/5000486-joni">Joni</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/820667.Jazz">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5000486-joni?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1876436560" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4792171373826261575">Synopsis: Joe shoots his 18-year-old girlfriend Dorcas in the shoulder at a party because she is dancing with another man after she has told him she is not interested in him anymore. The latter refuses help and bleeds to death. Joe’s wife, Violet, goes to the funeral and mutilates the corpse with a knife after which she is nicknamed “Violent”.<br/>Violet starts visiting Dorcas’s aunt Alice, and surprisingly, the two women become friends, bonding over a shared tragedy. The reader learns more about bot</span>
  <span id="freeText4792171373826261575" style="display:none">Synopsis: Joe shoots his 18-year-old girlfriend Dorcas in the shoulder at a party because she is dancing with another man after she has told him she is not interested in him anymore. The latter refuses help and bleeds to death. Joe’s wife, Violet, goes to the funeral and mutilates the corpse with a knife after which she is nicknamed “Violent”.<br />Violet starts visiting Dorcas’s aunt Alice, and surprisingly, the two women become friends, bonding over a shared tragedy. The reader learns more about both Joe and Violet’s histories through a web of details and flashbacks. We meet many characters, all having played important roles in shaping the main characters’ lives and we slip in and out of these people’s consciousness. Throughout the novel it remains unclear who is narrating the story.<br />The couple slowly starts to heal their relationship with the help of 18-year old Felice, Dorcas’s best friend. The three immediately hit it off and dine, wine and dance together. But doesn’t this relationship mirror the love triangle between Dorcas, Joe, and Violet….?<br /><br />Oh man, Toni Morrison. Where to begin. I think she’s my favorite writer who’s still alive. Jazz is the second book in Morrison’s Dantesque (Hell, Purgatory, Paradise) trilogy, the first book being Beloved (1987) and the last Paradise (1997). This is a wonderfully complicated book and it’s hard to even give a short summary because I feel like it doesn’t do justice to the complexity of the novel. It’s not as much about the events, as it is about perspective. About guilt, jealousy, love. About understanding the murderer as well as the murdered (Was it even murder or did she choose to die?). It is about justice. About what it’s like to be African American, human. Not once, and this is her strength, does Morrison pass judgment on any of the characters.<br /><br />The jazz scene of 1920s Harlem is a constant theme throughout the novel and the writing perfectly and beautifully mirrors the music: the characters shift from lamentations to sensual/sexual musings. Certain events are seen from various points of view, which in turn mirrors the call-and-response style typical of jazz music, in which a solo “call” is “responded” to by the ensemble. Moreover, Morrison borrows her similes and metaphors from the music as well:<br /><br />“… THE PAST WAS AN ABUSED RECORD WITH NO CHOICE BUT TO REPEAT ITSELF AT THE CRACK AND NO POWER ON EARTH COULD LIFT THE ARM THAT HELD THE NEEDLE.”<br /><br />Every single sentence flows like poetry and this is not an exaggeration. I started taking notes of quotes or passages that I liked to put in my review but ended up writing at least five things down from every page.<br /><br />I think a strong writer can say something without saying it. It’s all about the visuals, not the descriptions. Toni Morrison has a way of making you gasp for air, with the implications of what she’s saying:<br /><br />“… ALICE’S SISTER HAD JUST GOT THE NEWS AND HAD GONE BACK HOME TO TRY AND FORGET THE COLORS OF HIS ENTRAILS, WHEN HER HOUSE WAS TORCHED AND SHE BURNED CRISPY IN ITS FLAME. HER ONLY CHILD, A LITTLE GIRL NAMED DORCAS, SLEEPING ACROSS THE ROAD WITH HER VERY BEST GIRLFRIEND, DID NOT HEAR THE FIRE ENGINE CLANGING AND ROARING DOWN THE STREET BECAUSE WHEN IT WAS CALLED IT DIDN’T COME.”<br /><br />I loved the contrast between New York City and rural Virginia, which was beautifully symbolized by Violet’s caged birds and the Redwing birds in Virginia. Throughout the novel, the flight of the redwing birds suggest that Wild (a very dark-skinned woman living in the woods, presumed to be Joe’s mother) is near. This is a symbol recognized by the entire community. What this all symbolizes is that the birds in Virginia – representing Wild – still have their instinct to flight, while Violet’s birds – representing Violet of course – are trapped within society’s web. New York City might be vibrant and alive but it comes with a whole set of rules, especially if you’re part of a minority.<br /><br />Toni Morrison at her best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4792171373826261575" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Qa6IoL9p8h4LcKnBY2zfzR2efXTWt3Q94ZZITVmRDqbmUNyI0ATehGcAnEZRvTDzFEOS2FCqKjNjG0VQmjyqCw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

In The Body Artist, DeLillo tells the ha<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6458894853242828793\" style=\"display:none\">The Barnes &amp; Noble ReviewIn whatever form Don DeLillo chooses to write, there is simply no other American author who has so consistently pushed the boundaries of fiction in his effort to capture the zeitgeist. In The Body Artist, DeLillo tells the hallucinatory tale of performance artist Lauren Hartke in the days following the suicide of her husband, filmmaker Rey Robles. Finishing out their lease of a rented house on the coast, living in a self-imposed exile, Lauren discovers a mysterious man in the bedroom upstairs who is able to repeat -- verbatim -- entire conversations she had with her husband before his death but does not seem to know his own name or where he happens to come from.  \nDeLillo\'s emphasis on behavior and the inadequacies of language in The Body Artist will remind readers more of his plays (Valparaiso, The Day Room) than of his novels, and yet, in just a few pages -- 128, as compared to the sweeping, masterful Underworld\'s 800-plus -- DeLillo still manages to draw a rich portrait of contemporary American life in all its quotidian glory. Describing Lauren in the kitchen on the morning her husband will commit suicide, he writes, &quot;She took the kettle back to the stove because this is how you live a life even if you don\'t know it.&quot; In this opening scene, Lauren and Rey silently struggle to assign meaning and relevance to an ordinary moment. They have a routine; they know what comes next. But they can\'t say what it is. They seem cut off from their own actions. How do you articulate the emotion that accompanies eating breakfast with your spouse? As Rey puts it, &quot;I want to say something but what.&quot; When they finish eating, Rey drives to his ex-wife\'s apartment in Manhattan to kill himself.  \n\nThe question remains open as to whether or not the strange man (whom Lauren affectionately names Mr. Tuttle, after an English teacher of hers, when she finds him upstairs) exists at all, or if he is merely a figment of her imagination. But Mr. Tuttle\'s origins are entirely beside the point. He has no origins. He defies description. He is neither old nor young. &quot;Maybe this man experiences another kind of reality where he is here and there, before and after.&quot; And leave it to DeLillo to connect this enigma to the Internet. There is a live, 24-hour web site Lauren enjoys viewing: It shows an empty road in Kotka, Finland. Occasionally a car drives by or a person crosses the screen, but generally nothing happens. Lauren is fascinated by the notion that across the globe, at this very moment, this is happening, an episode &quot;real enough to withstand the circumstance of nothing going on.&quot; This may also be the best way to describe The Body Artist, a book in which &quot;it all happens around the word seem.&quot; \n\nIn DeLillo\'s unique brand of lucid, albeit elliptical, prose, The Body Artist addresses the very questions Gauguin inscribed on his famous painting: Who am I? Where do I come from? Where am I going? Lauren Hartke answers these questions by transforming the absurdities of her daily life -- that hours can seem long or short and still be hours; how a thing can look like something other than itself -- into a beautiful, suggestive live performance. Through her art, Lauren transcends the limits of language and body, approaching an understanding of her husband\'s death and more clearly discerning her own original nature. And in a brilliant act of spiritual ventriloquism, DeLillo, &quot;the poet of lonely places,&quot; dresses himself up in this character, placing us in the extreme situation of her search for an experience of meaning she can call living.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6458894853242828793\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div The Modern Library is proud to present Passing—an electrifying story of two women who cross the color line in 1920s New York—together with a new Introduction by the Obie Award- winning playwright and novelist Ntozake Shange.Irene Redfield, the novel\'s protagonist, is a woman with an enviable life. She and her husband, Brian, a prominent physician, share a comfortable Harlem town house with their sons. Her work arranging charity balls that gather Harlem\'s elite creates a sense of purpose and respectability for Irene. But her hold on this world begins to slip the day she encounters Clare Kendry, a childhood friend with whom she had lost touch. Clare—light-skinned, beautiful, and charming—tells Irene how, after her father\'s death, she left behind the black neighborhood of her adolescence and began passing for white, hiding her true identity from everyone, including her racist husband. As Clare begins inserting herself into Irene\'s life, Irene is thrown into a panic, terrified of the consequences of Clare\'s dangerous behavior. And when Clare witnesses the vibrancy and energy of the community she left behind, her burning desire to come back threatens to shatter her careful deception.\n\nBrilliantly plotted and elegantly Just who are these green women? They are powerful (one was NDiaye’s disciplinarian grade-school teacher). They are mysterious (one haunts a house like a ghost and may be visible only to the author). They are seductive (one stole a friend’s husband). And they are unbearably personal (one is NDiaye’s own mother). They are all, in their way, aspects of their creator, at once frightening, menacing, and revealing of everything submerged within the consciousness of this singular literary talent. A courageous, strikingly honest, and unabashedly innovative self-portrait, NDiaye’s kaleidoscopic look at the women in green is a revelation to us all — about how we form our identities, how we <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer509984550884040787">Toni Morrison (born Chloe Ardelia Wofford) was an American author, editor, and professor who won the 1993 Nobel Prize in Literature for being an author "who in novels characterized by visionary force and poetic import, gives life to an essential aspect of American reality." <br/><br/>Her novels are known for their epic themes, vivid dialogue, and richly detailed African American characters; among the best k</span>
  <span id="freeText509984550884040787" style="display:none">Toni Morrison (born Chloe Ardelia Wofford) was an American author, editor, and professor who won the 1993 Nobel Prize in Literature for being an author "who in novels characterized by visionary force and poetic import, gives life to an essential aspect of American reality." <br /><br />Her novels are known for their epic themes, vivid dialogue, and richly detailed African American characters; among the best known are her novels <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11337.The_Bluest_Eye" title="The Bluest Eye" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bluest Eye</a>
</i>, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11334.Song_of_Solomon" title="Song of Solomon" rel="nofollow noopener">Song of Solomon</a>
</i>, and <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved" title="Beloved" rel="nofollow noopener">Beloved</a>
</i>, which won the Pulitzer Prize for Fiction in 1988. In 2001 she was named one of "The 30 Most Powerful Women in America" by Ladies' Home Journal.<br /><br /></span>
